        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');

            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="93567b391de74fcfa055cb74ee81973d"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="93567b391de74fcfa055cb74ee81973d">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '279a1380-c579-4784-8d80-866fde1b0f19'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

        (function() {
            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {
                var store_domain = 'https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com';
                var session_id = '9B75DCD3A93090D5B6937C4AA9F5F1BF';
                var url = ('https:' == document.location.protocol ? 'https://' : 'http://')
                    + "beacon.riskified.com?shop=" + store_domain + "&sid=" + session_id;
                var s = document.createElement('script');
                s.type = 'text/javascript';
                s.async = true;
                s.src = url;
                var x = document.getElementsByTagName('script')[0];
                x.parentNode.insertBefore(s, x);
            }
            if (window.attachEvent)
                window.attachEvent('onload', riskifiedBeaconLoad)
            else
                window.addEventListener('load', riskifiedBeaconLoad, false);
        })();

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d4807515" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Recently, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have received considerable attention for large-scale energy storage applications. However, the low initial Coulombic efficiency of traditional SIBs severely impedes their further development. Here, a highly active Na<sub>2</sub>S-based composite is employed as a self-sacrificial additive for sodium compensation in SIBs. The in situ synthesized Na<sub>2</sub>S is wrapped in a carbon matrix with nanoscale particle size and good electrical conductivity, which helps it to achieve a significantly enhanced electrochemical activity as compare to commercial Na<sub>2</sub>S. As a highly efficient presodiation additive, the proposed Na<sub>2</sub>S/C composite can reach an initial charge capacity of 407&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. When 10&nbsp;wt.% Na<sub>2</sub>S/C additive is dispersed in the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode, and combined with a hard carbon anode, the full cell achieves 24.3% higher first discharge capacity, which corresponds to a 18.3% increase in the energy density from 117.2 to 138.6&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>. Meanwhile, it is found that the Na<sub>2</sub>S additive does not generate additional gas during the initial charging process, and under an appropriate content, its reaction product has no adverse impact on the cycling stability and rate performance of SIBs. Overall, this work establishes Na<sub>2</sub>S as a highly effective additive for the construction of advanced high-energy-density SIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202304793-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202304793-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as a promising candidate for next-generation energy storage systems due to their inexpensive cost and wide availability of Na element.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304793-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304793-bib-0001_R_d4807507e413" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Energy Mater.">1</a>, <a href="#smll202304793-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304793-bib-0002_R_d4807507e413" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Acta Phys.-Chim. Sin.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Similar to lithium-ion batteries, SIBs work by extracting Na<sup>+</sup> from the cathode into the electrolyte and then inserting it into the anode to realize the charging/discharging process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304793-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304793-bib-0003_R_d4807507e420" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nanotechnology">3</a>-<a href="#smll202304793-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304793-bib-0005_R_d4807507e420" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> All the sodium sources in the SIB system are supplied by the cathode. However, during the initial charging process, anodes such as hard carbon (HC) inevitably consume active Na<sup>+</sup> from the cathode by the formation of solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) film and some irreversible side reactions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304793-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304793-bib-0006_R_d4807507e428" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The sodium loss significantly reduces the overall energy density of SIBs. To this end, several strategies have been proposed to reduce the irreversible sodium loss in terms of material design and electrolyte optimization, but it is difficult to entirely eliminate the sodium consumption during the first cycle. In recent years, the presodiation of electrodes to compensate for the irreversible sodium loss has emerged as a vital approach to address this issue.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304793-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304793-bib-0007_R_d4807507e433" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">7</a>-<a href="#smll202304793-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304793-bib-0009_R_d4807507e433" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Energy Mater.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Generally, the presodiation strategies can be classified into three categories: physical presodiation, electrochemical presodiation, and introduction of additives. In the physical method, sodium metal is rolled into the electrode materials under the protection of Ar atmosphere.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304793-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304793-bib-0010_R_d4807507e440" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - J. Power Sources">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the highly active sodium metal violently reacts with oxygen when exposed to air, which limits the large-scale commercial applications of this strategy. In the electrochemical method, a homogeneous and stable SEI film is formed on the anode with an excellent presodiation effect. However, the cumbersome process significantly increases the implementation cost.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304793-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304793-bib-0011_R_d4807507e445" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Chem. Eng. J.">11</a>, <a href="#smll202304793-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304793-bib-0012_R_d4807507e445" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Solid State Chem.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the third strategy, the Na-containing cathode additives undergo electrochemical oxidation during the initial charge process, irreversibly releasing additional Na<sup>+</sup> into the reaction system.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304793-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304793-bib-0013_R_d4807507e453" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - ChemSusChem">13</a>-<a href="#smll202304793-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304793-bib-0015_R_d4807507e453" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among the three strategies, the introduction of cathode additives is considered to be the most promising presodiation method for commercial applications due to its cost effectiveness, easy operation, and high safety.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304793-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304793-bib-0016_R_d4807507e458" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Chem. Commun.">16</a>, <a href="#smll202304793-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304793-bib-0017_R_d4807507e458" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Chem. Eng. J.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Until now, Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304793-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304793-bib-0018_R_d4807507e468" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Chem. Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304793-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304793-bib-0019_R_d4807507e480" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Mater.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>4</sub>O<sub>4</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304793-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304793-bib-0020_R_d4807507e491" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Energy Storage Mater.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> NaNO<sub>2</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304793-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304793-bib-0021_R_d4807507e499" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J. Mater. Chem. A">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Na<sub>3</sub>P,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304793-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304793-bib-0022_R_d4807507e507" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nat. Commun.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and NaN<sub>3</sub><sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304793-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304793-bib-0023_R_d4807507e513" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Power Sources">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> have been investigated as self-sacrificial sodium compensation additives for SIBs. However, there are still some drawbacks need to be addressed. For example, Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, and NaNO<sub>2</sub> will produce a large amount of gases and increase pressure due to the inflation inside the battery.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304793-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304793-bib-0024_R_d4807507e531" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Power Sources">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, these materials with low theoretical specific capacity can provide a limited amount of sodium ions. Na<sub>3</sub>P and Na<sub>3</sub>N are desirable sodium compensation additives of cathode because of their high theoretical specific capacity without additional gas production.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304793-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304793-bib-0022_R_d4807507e541" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nat. Commun.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As a similar type of sodium salt, Na<sub>2</sub>S is a good sodium compensation additive because of its high theoretical specific capacity of 687&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and stable oxidative decomposition.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304793-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304793-bib-0025_R_d4807507e551" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Chem. - Eur. J.">25</a>-<a href="#smll202304793-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304793-bib-0027_R_d4807507e551" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Energy Mater.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> More importantly, the Na<sub>2</sub>S has been utilized for sodium extraction at an oxidation potential of ≈3.2&nbsp;V, which facilitates complete decomposition in the voltage window of cathode in SIBs, with high utilization of Na<sub>2</sub>S for sodium replenishment.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304793-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304793-bib-0028_R_d4807507e560" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">28</a>, <a href="#smll202304793-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304793-bib-0029_R_d4807507e560" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Chin. J. Chem. Eng.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, commercial anhydrous Na<sub>2</sub>S has large particles and low electronic conductivity, resulting in the inability to extract active sodium ions effectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304793-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304793-bib-0030_R_d4807507e568" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Energy Storage Mater.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, the development of advanced Na<sub>2</sub>S additives to realize presodiation of SIBs is of immense practical significance.</p>
         
         <p>In this study, we propose an effective strategy to convert cost-effective Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> into Na<sub>2</sub>S through a carbothermic reduction reaction. The in situ generated Na<sub>2</sub>S is distributed in the carbon network formed by the carbonization of polyvinylpyrrolidone (PVP). Further, it has a higher electrical conductivity and smaller particle size than commercial Na<sub>2</sub>S. The results reveal that its maximum specific presodiation capacity can reach to 407&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> when the Na<sub>2</sub>S content is 75.8% (denoted as Na<sub>2</sub>S−4) Accordingly, Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 is incorporated as a compensation additive into the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) cathode. The performance of a full cell containing NVP cathode with 5−15&nbsp;wt.% Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 additive and HC anode is investigated, and the NVP cathode containing 10&nbsp;wt.% Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 additive is obtained to be the optimal composition based on the energy density of the cell. Notably, the addition of Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 effectively offsets the sodium loss and enhances the energy density of the full cell, demonstrating immense application potential for constructing advanced SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202304793-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202304793-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The in situ synthesis of Na<sub>2</sub>S is based on the carbothermic reduction reaction of Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> and carbon. First, Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> precursor was mixed with PVP and Ketjen black (KB) to form a Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub>/PVP/KB composite in anhydrous ethanol. Followed by the heat treatment process under H<sub>2</sub>/Ar flow, Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> was reduced to Na<sub>2</sub>S by the carbon source. The obtained Na<sub>2</sub>S was uniformly encapsulated in the carbon framework. The synthetic procedure of Na<sub>2</sub>S is depicted in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). When the amount of Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> was 3, 4, 5, and 6&nbsp;g, the products obtained by carbon reduction were denoted as Na<sub>2</sub>S−3, Na<sub>2</sub>S−4, Na<sub>2</sub>S−5, and Na<sub>2</sub>S−6, respectively. Their X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns are in good agreement with the standard crystal phase of Na<sub>2</sub>S (PDF#77-2149), suggesting that the pure Na<sub>2</sub>S phase is successfully synthesized (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202304793-fig-0001">1a</a>). Meanwhile, the intensity of Na<sub>2</sub>S peaks gradually increases with the increase in the amount of Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub>. The content of Na<sub>2</sub>S in the composite was determined through the elemental analysis method. As illustrated in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information), the S content in Na<sub>2</sub>S−3, Na<sub>2</sub>S−4, Na<sub>2</sub>S−5, and Na<sub>2</sub>S−6 is 25.2%, 31.7%, 35.0%, and 39.7%, respectively. Based on the mass fraction of S in Na<sub>2</sub>S, the content of Na<sub>2</sub>S in Na<sub>2</sub>S−3, Na<sub>2</sub>S−4, Na<sub>2</sub>S−5, and Na<sub>2</sub>S−6 content are calculated to be 61.4%,75.8%, 85.3%, and 96.9%, which are consistent with the XRD results (Details of the calculation are in the Supporting Information). The Raman spectrum of in situ synthesized Na<sub>2</sub>S has two well-defined peaks at 1354 and 1601&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> corresponding to the disorder-induced amorphous carbon (D-band) and ordered graphite lattice (G-band), which indicate the presence of defect−rich carbon and highly conductive graphitized carbon in the synthesized Na<sub>2</sub>S.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304793-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304793-bib-0031_R_d4807507e716" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Nano Energy">31</a>, <a href="#smll202304793-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304793-bib-0032_R_d4807507e716" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Appl. Surf. Sci.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, the in situ synthesized Na<sub>2</sub>S was detected to the S<sup>2−</sup> corresponding to the Na<sub>2</sub>S component.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304793-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304793-bib-0033_R_d4807507e728" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Nat. Commun.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202304793-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4e2321df-98c1-4664-bb9c-899b5a5199d4/smll202304793-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/4e2321df-98c1-4664-bb9c-899b5a5199d4/smll202304793-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4c7f76e3-4fde-47c6-a4fd-7db80e1254ee/smll202304793-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4e2321df-98c1-4664-bb9c-899b5a5199d4/smll202304793-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202304793-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202304793" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) XRD patterns, b) Raman spectrums, c) first charge/discharge curves of Na<sub>2</sub>S−3, Na<sub>2</sub>S−4, Na<sub>2</sub>S−5, and Na<sub>2</sub>S−6. d) SEM image, e) TEM image, and f) SEM image corresponding to EDS elemental mappings of Na<sub>2</sub>S−4. g) The CV curves of Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 at an initial cycle. h) Ex situ XRD patterns of the Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 electrode before charge and first charge to different voltages. i) High-resolution XPS spectra of S 2p of the Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 electrode before, after charge to 4&nbsp;V (C4V), and discharge to 2.5&nbsp;V (D2.5&nbsp;V).</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The in situ synthesized Na<sub>2</sub>S was assembled in a half cell to explore its ability for sodium sources extraction during the charging process. The first charge–discharge curves of cell at different ratios of Na<sub>2</sub>S are displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304793-fig-0001">1c</a>. The broad charge plateau is ≈3.8&nbsp;V. The Na<sub>2</sub>S−3, Na<sub>2</sub>S−4, Na<sub>2</sub>S−5, and Na<sub>2</sub>S−6 samples deliver an initial charge specific capacity of 320, 407, 298, and 263&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, in which Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 has the highest charge specific capacity. Meanwhile, the discharge capacity of Na<sub>2</sub>S−3, Na<sub>2</sub>S−4, Na<sub>2</sub>S−5, and Na<sub>2</sub>S−6 is only 36, 39, 27, and 16&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, suggesting that Na<sub>2</sub>S extracts abundant sodium ions during the charging process, while it does not consume sodium ions during the discharging process. Then, the electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) was employed to evaluate the electron transfer capability of Na<sub>2</sub>S−3, Na<sub>2</sub>S−4, Na<sub>2</sub>S−5, Na<sub>2</sub>S−6, and commercial Na<sub>2</sub>S, respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). The charge transfer resistance <i>(R<sub>ct</sub></i>) of commercial Na<sub>2</sub>S is significantly larger than those of in situ synthesized Na<sub>2</sub>S. In addition, the internal resistance of these four Na<sub>2</sub>S samples are close (&lt;5&nbsp;Ω), and the <i>R<sub>ct</sub></i> slightly increases with the decrease in carbon content. Since commercial Na<sub>2</sub>S has poor electrochemical activity, and it only exhibits an initial charge/discharge specific capacity of 38/5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information), it could not serve as the persodiation additive. Therefore, the Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 sample, which shows the best sodium source utilization of 59% among all samples, is considered as a suitable sodium supplement additive.</p>
         
         <p>The scanning electron microscopy (SEM) image of Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 shows a rough surface with no aggregation, where the particle size of the Na<sub>2</sub>S/C composite is in the micron range (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304793-fig-0001">1d</a>). The SEM image of commercial Na<sub>2</sub>S in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information) shows that the particles are bulky and clumped together. The distribution of carbon in Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 is examined through transmission electron microscopy (TEM), as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304793-fig-0001">1e</a>. A carbon layer is attached to the surface of Na<sub>2</sub>S−4, which is formed due to the carbonization of PVP. The presence of this carbon layer enhances the electrical conductivity of Na<sub>2</sub>S−4, thereby increasing its electrochemical activity. Moreover, the SEM image and the corresponding energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS) elemental mapping results reveal the uniform distribution of C, Na, and S in the Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 sample (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304793-fig-0001">1f</a>). To clarify the electrochemical behavior of Na<sub>2</sub>S−4, we performed cyclic voltammetry (CV) tests for the first three cycles, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304793-fig-0001">1g</a>. In the first cathodic scan, the oxidation of Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 starts at 3&nbsp;V and becomes most intense at 3.8&nbsp;V, which can be ascribed to the sodium ion extraction process. The absence of a reduction peak indicates that the generation of sodium ions from Na<sub>2</sub>S is an irreversible process. The lack of redox peaks in the two subsequent CV cycles further verifies that the decomposition of Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 is extremely irreversible. The above results reveal that Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 provides a sufficient sodium source to be an excellent candidate for sodium supplementation additive. Ex situ XRD and Raman spectroscopy were employed to understand the dynamic process of sodium ion release from Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304793-fig-0001">1&nbsp;h</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). As the charging potential increases from 2.5 to 4.0&nbsp;V, the gradual disappearance of the Na<sub>2</sub>S peaks indicates its decomposition. Similarly, the characteristic Raman peak of the Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 fresh cell at 433&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> was analyzed as S<sup>2−</sup>, while it gradually diminishes when the charging process starts. A weak peak emerges at 480&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> after charging to ≈3.7&nbsp;V continuously to 4&nbsp;V, which is ascribed to S.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304793-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304793-bib-0034_R_d4807507e908" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv. Energy Mater.">34</a>, <a href="#smll202304793-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304793-bib-0035_R_d4807507e908" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, the oxidative decomposition of Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 can be described as Na<sub>2</sub>S→2Na<sup>+</sup> + S + 2e<sup>−</sup>. The ex situ X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) technique was employed to further confirm the initial cycling product of Na<sub>2</sub>S−4. The high-resolution S 2p spectrum in the fresh state shows three couples corresponding to S<sup>2−</sup> (Na−S), S−S, and sulfate (SO<sub>x</sub>) species, respectively<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304793-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304793-bib-0036_R_d4807507e929" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Adv. Mater.">36</a>, <a href="#smll202304793-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304793-bib-0037_R_d4807507e929" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Adv. Mater.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304793-fig-0001">1i</a>). After the first charging and discharging process, the peaks of S<sup>2−</sup> disappear due to the oxidative decomposition of Na<sub>2</sub>S−4. The weak S−S peaks exist in the charged state, probably due to the amorphous form of S after the extraction of sodium ions. The SO<sub>x</sub> peak becomes significantly stronger in the discharged state, indicating that Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 is mainly present in the electrode as SO<sub>x</sub>/C (Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 contains nearly 15&nbsp;wt.% carbon according to the Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information) at the end of sodium replenishment, which also verifies the irreversible decomposition of Na<sub>2</sub>S−4.</p>
         
         <p>Additionally, the SEM images of the Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 electrode in the pristine state, after initial charging to 4&nbsp;V and after initial discharging to 2.5&nbsp;V are shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202304793-fig-0002">2a−c</a> (Supporting Information). After charging, the morphology of Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 particles changes dramatically and distinct holes with a size of ≈100&nbsp;nm appear, which is ascribed to the gradual decomposition of Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 driven by the voltage to extract the active sodium ions. After further discharge to 2.5&nbsp;V, the Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 electrode still retains the pore structure. The gas production behavior during battery operation is an important indicator of battery safety. In situ differential electrochemical mass spectrometry (DEMS) was employed to investigate the composition of the gases during the first sodium supplementation of the Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 sample. After resting for 2&nbsp;h to pass He, the response ion current of CO and CO<sub>2</sub> starts to appear when Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 is charged for 10&nbsp;h (to ≈3.7&nbsp;V), and the ion current gradually increases as the charging continues. H<sub>2</sub> is consistently produced throughout the cycle. The H<sub>2</sub>, CO, and CO<sub>2</sub> may originate from the decomposition of the carbonate electrolyte.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304793-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304793-bib-0038_R_d4807507e986" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The absence of ionic currents for O<sub>2</sub>, H<sub>2</sub>S, and SO<sub>2</sub> indicates that no such gases are produced (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304793-fig-0002">2d</a>). Furthermore, an ultrasonic scanning technique developed by our group was utilized to analyze the comprehensive gas generation process inside the Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 || Cu pouch cell, which only includes the Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 electrode material, and the influence of other materials that might produce gases was ignored. A digital image of Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 || Cu pouch cell is displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304793-fig-0002">2e</a>. The blue color in the transmission mapping image represents a low ultrasonic transmittance, indicating the gas generation behavior. In the fresh state, the relatively high ultrasonic transmittance indicates no gas generation (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304793-fig-0002">2f</a>). After the first charge to 4&nbsp;V and discharge to 2.5&nbsp;V, the localized blue color of the electrode region in the transmission mapping image indicates that the initial cycling process of the Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 || Cu pouch cell may be accompanied by gas generation (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304793-fig-0002">2g and h</a>). In combination with the DEMS analysis, it is observed that the gases produced in the first cycle are CO, CO<sub>2</sub>, and H<sub>2</sub>, which is probably due to the decomposition of the electrolyte unrelated to the Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 additive. This verifies the non−gas producing nature of the Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 additive.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202304793-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a5a649e8-9cb2-4465-9c67-dee687171693/smll202304793-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/a5a649e8-9cb2-4465-9c67-dee687171693/smll202304793-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4d97f7b0-bf4c-46c4-9189-45297ac12b2a/smll202304793-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a5a649e8-9cb2-4465-9c67-dee687171693/smll202304793-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202304793-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202304793" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">SEM images of Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 electrode a) in fresh state, b,c) after first charge, and discharge states. d) Detection of simultaneous gas release variation curves in the In situ DEMS tests of the first charge/discharge cycle for Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 half cell. e) The digital photograph of Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 || Cu pouch cell. Ultrasonic transmission mapping of the Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 || Cu pouch cells in f) fresh state, g) after first charge to 4&nbsp;V, and h) after first discharge to 2.5&nbsp;V.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>According to the above analysis, Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 can provide abundant electrochemically active sodium ions without generating gaseous by-products. Therefore, based on the mass of NVP&nbsp;active materials, we blended 5−15&nbsp;wt.% Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 as a self−sacrificial sodium compensation additives into commercial NVP material, and these cathodes were denoted as NVP−5%Na<sub>2</sub>S−4, NVP−10%Na<sub>2</sub>S−4, and NVP−15%Na<sub>2</sub>S−4. The XRD pattern of NVP powder is presented in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information), and the inset shows the corresponding SEM image with a particle size of ≈5−10&nbsp;µm. The SEM images of the electrodes containing 0−15&nbsp;wt.% Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 additive are displayed in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7−S10</a> (Supporting Information), respectively. Larger NVP particles can be observed on the surface of these electrode. The EDS elemental mappings dmonstrate even distribution of S element in these electrodes, indicating a uniform dispersion of the Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 additive during the electrode preparation. The S content in the NVP−5%Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 (0.67&nbsp;wt.%), NVP−10%Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 (1.80&nbsp;wt.%), and NVP−15%Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 (3.47&nbsp;wt.%) electrodes increases sequentially. Meanwhile, the mass ratio of all the elements (C, Na, S, V, and P) were listed in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information). The Na content in the NVP−5%Na<sub>2</sub>S−4, NVP−10%Na<sub>2</sub>S−4, and NVP−15%Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 electrodes is 12.30, 14.82, and 16.19&nbsp;wt.%, further proving that the higher the amount of Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 additive, the higher the amount of supplemented sodium ions.</p>
         
         <p>The electrochemical performances of the presodiation electrodes were tested by pairing them with the Na metal to assemble the half cells. The charge and discharge curves of the electrodes with 0−15&nbsp;wt.% Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 additive are presented in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202304793-fig-0003">3a</a>. The raw NVP electrode delivers the first specific charge/discharge capacity of 115/112&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.2&nbsp;C (1&nbsp;C = 117&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>). The NVP electrodes with 5−15&nbsp;wt.% Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 additive have an initial specific charge/discharge capacity of 126/113, 133/113, and 149/114&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The additional charge capacity of 11−34&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> originates from the decomposition of 5−15&nbsp;wt.% Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 additive. The peak areas of the CV curves for NVP electrodes with 0−15&nbsp;wt.% Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 additive are calculated to be 0.0836, 0.0855, 0.0872, and 0.0901, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304793-fig-0003">3b</a>). The sequential increase in the CV peak area is consistent with the sequential increase in the first charge capacity. The cycling and rate performance were shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304793-fig-0003">3c and d</a>. The four electrodes show charge capacities of 107&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> for NVP, 110&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> for NVP−5%Na<sub>2</sub>S−4, 111&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> for NVP−10%Na<sub>2</sub>S−4, and 112&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> for NVP−15%Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 after 150 cycles with excellent cycle stability, respectively. In terms of rate performance, at the current density of 4&nbsp;C, the NVP, NVP−5%Na<sub>2</sub>S−4, NVP−10%Na<sub>2</sub>S−4, and NVP−15%Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 electrodes maintain a similar capacity of ≈105&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. The long cycling performance at 1&nbsp;C is displayed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> (Supporting Information). The NVP electrode with 0−15&nbsp;wt.%Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 retains &gt;85% decent capacity retention after 500 cycles. The electrochemical performance of the NVP electrode with 5−15&nbsp;wt.% Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 additive is no different from that without the additive, demonstrating that the Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 additive has no detrimental effect on the electrochemical performance of NVP half-cells. The EIS measurement was performed on the fresh electrode and the electrodes in the first charge and discharge state. The <i>R<sub>ct</sub></i> of the fresh state increases with the increase in the amount of Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 additive, which can be attributed to the slightly inferior electrical conductivity of the Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 additive (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information). In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304793-fig-0003">3e and f</a>, the <i>R<sub>ct</sub></i> of the NVP with 5 and 10&nbsp;wt.% Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 additive decreases after the first charge and discharge process because Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 may be in an activated state after the extraction of sodium ions to facilitate the transfer of electrons. According to the ex situ XPS analysis of Na<sub>2</sub>S−4, SO<sub>x</sub>/C is the end product of the additive after the first cycle. The combination of highly conductive carbon substrate and SO<sub>x</sub> has a minor effect on the charge transfer of NVP electrode. However, when 15&nbsp;wt.% Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 is added, the <i>R<sub>ct</sub></i> of the battery always remains at a high value, indicating that such an amount of additive reduces the electron transport in the NVP composite electrode. Therefore, the EIS data suggests that the charge transfer in the NVP electrode can be hindered if the amount of Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 additive is ≈15&nbsp;wt.%.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202304793-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/742e63d9-6133-48de-b4e9-739705bd9e44/smll202304793-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/742e63d9-6133-48de-b4e9-739705bd9e44/smll202304793-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ccbac5b7-3bcf-4fbd-a4b3-e509c3774153/smll202304793-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/742e63d9-6133-48de-b4e9-739705bd9e44/smll202304793-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202304793-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202304793" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of the NVP, NVP−5%Na<sub>2</sub>S−4, NVP−10%Na<sub>2</sub>S−4, and NVP−15%Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 electrode in half cells: a) charge and discharge curves; b) CV curves; c) cycling performance; d) rate property. e,f) EIS Nyquist plots after the first charge/discharge process.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <div class="paragraph-element">To better investigate the sodium ion transfer capability after the introduction of Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 additive, the sodium ion diffusion coefficient (D<sub>Na+</sub>) of NVP electrode with 0−15&nbsp;wt.% Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 additive is calculated through the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT). When the battery is stabilized after 5 cycles, a current pulse at 0.2&nbsp;C is applied for 15&nbsp;min with a subsequent relaxing time of 2&nbsp;h to reach the potential equilibrium state.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304793-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304793-bib-0039_R_d4807507e1237" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Adv. Energy Mater.">39</a>, <a href="#smll202304793-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304793-bib-0040_R_d4807507e1237" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Adv. Energy Mater.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The GITT curves of NVP, NVP−5%Na<sub>2</sub>S−4, NVP−10%Na<sub>2</sub>S−4, and NVP−15%Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 electrodes are displayed in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202304793-fig-0004">4a−c</a>. The value of D<sub>Na+</sub> can be calculated as follows: <sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304793-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304793-bib-0041_R_d4807507e1258" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Sci.">41</a>, <a href="#smll202304793-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304793-bib-0042_R_d4807507e1258" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Adv. Mater.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup><div class="inline-equation" id="smll202304793-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/f998c7fd-595f-4409-a38a-d6ae41bf2873/smll202304793-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16136810:media:smll202304793:smll202304793-math-0001" display="block" location="graphic/smll202304793-math-0001.png">

         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202304793-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9ea26907-95e6-4fac-be2a-d5305b733aed/smll202304793-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/9ea26907-95e6-4fac-be2a-d5305b733aed/smll202304793-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ee96a1e4-eefb-43d1-a383-cec8bd26e328/smll202304793-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9ea26907-95e6-4fac-be2a-d5305b733aed/smll202304793-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202304793-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202304793" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a−c) Charge/discharge profiles via the GITT technique and d) the corresponding Na-ion diffusion coefficients of NVP, NVP−5%Na<sub>2</sub>S−4, NVP−10%Na<sub>2</sub>S−4, and NVP−15%Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 electrodes.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The dynamic variations in the D<sub>Na+</sub> of NVP, NVP−5%Na<sub>2</sub>S−4, NVP−10%Na<sub>2</sub>S−4, and NVP−15%Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 electrodes are presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304793-fig-0004">4d</a>. The D<sub>Na+</sub> of the NVP electrode is in the range of 10<sup>−7</sup>−10<sup>−8</sup>&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>, which is slightly higher than that of the samples with 5−15&nbsp;wt.% Na<sub>2</sub>S additive (10<sup>−8</sup>−10<sup>−9</sup>&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>), suggesting that the reaction by-product of Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 hinders the migration of sodium ions to some extent. It is well known that the NVP is a typical sodium superionic conductor (NASICON) with rapid sodium ion conduction. The smaller D<sub>Na+</sub> for the presodiation electrode may be because the decomposition product SO<sub>x</sub>/C of the Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 additive resists the sodium ion migration. Besides, the D<sub>Na+</sub> values of the presodiation NVP electrodes with 5−15&nbsp;wt.% Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 additive are somewhat different in the early stages of charging and discharging, but they are similar in the later stages. The Na<sub>2</sub>S content of 5−15&nbsp;wt.% in the NVP electrode has a minor effect on the sodium ion migration. Therefore, based on the GITT technique, it can be concluded that the Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 additive has a negligible effect on the sodium ion diffusion in the NVP electrode.</p>
         
         <p>The ex situ SEM images of the persodiation electrode were obtained to analyze the effect of Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 additive on the electrode structure. After the first charge to 4&nbsp;V and discharge to 2.5&nbsp;V, the NVP component is visible, and intact particles without rupture are observed on the surface of NVP (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information), NVP−5%Na<sub>2</sub>S−4(<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202304793-fig-0005">5a and d</a>), NVP−10%Na<sub>2</sub>S−4(Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304793-fig-0005">5b and e</a>), and NVP−15%Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304793-fig-0005">5c and f</a>) electrodes. The NVP electrodes containing 0−15&nbsp;wt.% Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 additive have essentially the same morphology after the first cycle, which demonstrates that incorporation of Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 additive can lead to a presodiation effect while hardly affecting the original structure of the NVP material. We concluded from the above EIS, GITT, ex situ SEM analysis that a certain amount of Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 additive has no adverse effect on the electron and ion transport and electrode structure of NVP half-cells. Mechanistic diagram of the Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 sodium replenishment process is displayed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a> (Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202304793-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/95160b54-3883-4e2e-97fb-00fc55b462dc/smll202304793-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/95160b54-3883-4e2e-97fb-00fc55b462dc/smll202304793-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/23a7eb41-9683-4149-a09e-e6832c413287/smll202304793-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/95160b54-3883-4e2e-97fb-00fc55b462dc/smll202304793-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202304793-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202304793" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">SEM images after initial charge and discharge states of a,d) NVP−5%Na<sub>2</sub>S−4, b,e) NVP−10%Na<sub>2</sub>S−4, and c,f) NVP−15%Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 electrodes.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To evaluate the effectiveness of Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 additive on offset the initial sodium loss in SIBs, full cells were assembled using NVP cathodes with 0−15&nbsp;wt.% of Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 and commercial HC anode. The configuration and presodiation mechanism are shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202304793-fig-0006">6a</a>. The XRD pattern, morphology, and electrochemical performance of the HC anode are displayed in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a> (Supporting Information). The HC anode in the half cell delivers initial charge/discharge specific capacities of 302/362&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with an initial Coulombic efficiency of 83.4%, and the cell remains outstanding cycling stability. The HC anode consumes sodium ions due to the formation of SEI film and irreversible side reactions during the first charge and discharge process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202304793-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304793-bib-0043_R_d4807507e1475" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">43</a>, <a href="#smll202304793-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202304793-bib-0044_R_d4807507e1475" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the full cell assembly, the NVP paired with HC anode (NVP || HC) exhibits an initial discharge specific capacity of 70&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with an initial Coulombic efficiency of 60.3% at a current density of 0.2&nbsp;C. The charge/discharge specific capacities of NVP−5%Na<sub>2</sub>S−4, NVP−10%Na<sub>2</sub>S−4, and NVP−15%Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 full cells are 137/79, 148/87, and 167/78&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively which are significantly higher than that of the NVP||HC full cell (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304793-fig-0006">6b</a>). The NVP−10%Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 || HC full cell exhibits the largest CV peak area, which is consistent with its highest discharge specific capacity (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304793-fig-0006">6c</a>). Notably, for NVP−15%Na<sub>2</sub>S−4||HC, the reduction peak current of ≈3.2&nbsp;V is sharply reduced, which may be because the 15&nbsp;wt.% Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 additive affects the discharge capacity of HC in the full cell. The rate performance was tested at the current densities of 0.2 to 4&nbsp;C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304793-fig-0006">6d</a>). Among the different samples, after 10 cycles of stabilization, the NVP−10%Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 || HC demonstrates the best rate performance with specific capacity of 78, 72, 67, 62, and 57&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. When the current density is restored to 0.2&nbsp;C, the specific capacity of NVP−10%Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 || HC reaches 73&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. The charge/discharge profiles of these four full cells at various current densities are depicted in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a> (Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202304793-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a978da5f-ffdc-42ea-b27d-91853c6a2255/smll202304793-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/a978da5f-ffdc-42ea-b27d-91853c6a2255/smll202304793-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1685dbb8-791a-4e04-8586-74395cf9f57a/smll202304793-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a978da5f-ffdc-42ea-b27d-91853c6a2255/smll202304793-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202304793-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202304793" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Schematic illustration of NVP−Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 || HC full cell. Electrochemical performance of the NVP, NVP−5%Na<sub>2</sub>S−4, NVP−10%Na<sub>2</sub>S−4, and NVP−15%Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 electrode in full cells: b) charge and discharge curves; c) CV curves; d) rate capability and e) prolonged cycle performance. f) Charge and discharge profiles in the initial cycle and g) cycling performance of high loading NVP−10%Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 electrode in full cell. h) Energy density comparison of no additive, 5% additive, 10% additive, and 15% additive in full cells.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Furthermore, the long−term cycling stability of the four full cells was measured at a current density of 1&nbsp;C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304793-fig-0006">6e</a>). As expected, the NVP−10%Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 || HC full cell delivers a high discharge specific capacity of 87&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> in the first cycle at 0.2&nbsp;C activation and still retains a reversible capacity of 54&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 500 cycles at 1&nbsp;C, which is significantly higher than these of NVP || HC (37&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), NVP−5%Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 || HC (45&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), and NVP−15%Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 || HC (8&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) full cells. The capacity of NVP−15%Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 || HC decreases sharply before 100 cycles with a capacity retention of only 20%, which might be mainly caused by the limitation of ionic and electronic conductivity of Na<sub>2</sub>S in Na<sub>2</sub>S−4. The addition of 10&nbsp;wt.% Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 additive in the NVP cathode provides the right amount of additional sodium sources to increase the battery capacity as well as to ensure the cycling stability of the full cell. Therefore, 10&nbsp;wt.% is the optimum content of Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 additive in the NVP cathode, which is consistent with the commercial HC full cell configuration. The EIS results of the full cells are depicted in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a> (Supporting Information). The internal resistance increases with the increase in the content of Na<sub>2</sub>S additive in the fresh state due to the poor electrical conductivity of the additive. After the first charge and discharge cycle, the <i>R<sub>ct</sub></i> of the full cell with 10&nbsp;wt.% Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 additive is less than that of the cell with 0, 5, and 15&nbsp;wt.% additive. This is probably because the 10&nbsp;wt.% Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 in the full cell is completely decomposed in an activated amorphous state, facilitating the rapid charge transportation. Therefore, 10&nbsp;wt.% Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 additive is beneficial to the electrochemical performance of the full cell.</p>
         
         <p>To further confirm the applicability of Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 as a presodiation cathode additive, full cells were assembled with the higher loading of NVP (≈8&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>) and 10&nbsp;wt.% Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 additive. The high loading of NVP−10%Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 electrode in the half cell results in an initial charge/discharge specific capacity of 125/104&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and the cell maintains favorable cycling stability with a discharge capacity retention rate of 93% after 150 cycles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, Supporting Information). As presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304793-fig-0006">6f</a>, the full cell with high NVP loading delivers the initial charge/discharge specific capacity of 137/81&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with a discharge plateau of ≈3.3&nbsp;V. The cycling performance test results reveal a reversible discharge capacity of 54&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 110 cycles and a discharge capacity retention of 66% at 0.2&nbsp;C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304793-fig-0006">6g</a>). The persodiation electrode with NVP loading of 8&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> exhibits a slight reduction in the specific capacity along with favorable cycling stability, indicating its potential for practical application under high-loading requirements. Based on the total mass of the cathode and anode, the energy density of the full cell was calculated, and the results are displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202304793-fig-0006">6h</a>. The energy density of NVP || HC without additive is 117.2&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;Kg<sup>−1</sup>. When the amount of Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 additive is 5, 10, and 15&nbsp;wt.%, the corresponding energy densities are 130.6, 138.6, and 122.8&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;Kg<sup>−1</sup>. The full cell with 10&nbsp;wt.% Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 additive has the highest energy density, ≈18.3% higher than that of the cell without additive. Therefore, it can be concluded that the addition of 10&nbsp;wt.% Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 maximizes the energy density of the full cell while maintaining its stable operation. Among the reported presodiation additives for cathode, the Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 additive obtained by the reduction of Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> provides outstanding advantages such as high capacity for Na compensation, no gas generation, and facile operation. Therefore, the proposed Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 is a promising additive for sodium supplementation to improve the capacity and energy density of advanced SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202304793-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202304793-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In conclusion, a highly active Na<sub>2</sub>S/C composite was successfully prepared, and when it was used as an additive in the cathode, the initial Coulombic efficiency of SIBs was significantly enhanced. When 10&nbsp;wt.% Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 was added in the NVP cathode, the diffusion kinetics of sodium ions and electrons were negligibly affected, and the electrochemical performance of the Na-ion full cells was not deteriorated. Consequently, NVP−10%Na<sub>2</sub>S−4 || HC full cell achieved an energy density of ≈138.6&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>, which was 18.3% higher than that of full cell without additive. Overall, this work demonstrates the excellent potential of Na<sub>2</sub>S as a presodiation additive for cathode and provides useful insights on the incorporation of highly active additives to achieve high-performance SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
